<p>Metadata for scientific publications is often created by journal publishers and citation databases such as <a href="/wiki/PubMed" title="PubMed">PubMed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Web_of_Science" title="Web of Science">Web of Science</a>. The data contained within manuscripts or accompanying them as supplementary material is less often subject to metadata creation,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> though they may be submitted to e.g. biomedical databases after publication. The original authors and database curators then become responsible for metadata creation, with the assistance of automated processes. Comprehensive metadata for all experimental data is the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/FAIR_data" title="FAIR data">FAIR Guiding Principles</a>, or the standards for ensuring research data are <a href="/wiki/Findability" title="Findability">findable</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accessibility" title="Accessibility">accessible</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interoperability" title="Interoperability">interoperable</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reusability" title="Reusability">reusable</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Such metadata can then be utilized, complemented, and made accessible in useful ways. <a href="/wiki/Our_Research#OpenAlex" class="mw-redirect" title="Our Research">OpenAlex</a> is a free online index of over 200 million scientific documents that integrates and provides metadata such as sources, <a href="/wiki/Scientific_citation" title="Scientific citation">citations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Academic_authorship" title="Academic authorship">author information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scientific_field" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific field">scientific fields</a>, and research topics. Its <a href="/wiki/API" title="API">API</a> and open source website can be used for metascience, <a href="/wiki/Scientometrics" title="Scientometrics">scientometrics</a>, and novel tools that query this <a href="/wiki/Semantic_Web" title="Semantic Web">semantic</a> web of <a href="/wiki/Academic_paper" class="mw-redirect" title="Academic paper">papers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another project under development, <a href="/wiki/Scholia" title="Scholia">Scholia</a>, uses the metadata of scientific publications for various visualizations and aggregation features such as providing a simple user interface summarizing literature about a specific feature of the SARS-CoV-2 virus using <a href="/wiki/Wikidata" title="Wikidata">Wikidata</a>'s "main subject" property.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10.1186/s12915-020-00940-y&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10.1186/s12915-020-00940-y-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In research labor, transparent metadata about authors' contributions to works have been proposed – e.g. the role played in the production of the paper, the level of contribution and the responsibilities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Moreover, various metadata about scientific outputs can be created or complemented – for instance, some organizations attempt to track and link citations of papers as 'Supporting', 'Mentioning' or 'Contrasting' the study.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other examples include developments of <a href="/wiki/Altmetrics" title="Altmetrics">alternative metrics</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-10.1126/science.aao0185&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10.1126/science.aao0185-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> – which, beyond providing help for assessment and findability, also aggregate many of the public discussions about a scientific paper on social media such as <a href="/wiki/Reddit" title="Reddit">Reddit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science_information_on_Wikipedia" title="Science information on Wikipedia">citations on Wikipedia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Science_communication" title="Science communication">reports about the study</a> in the news media<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> – and a call for showing <a href="/wiki/Reproducibility" title="Reproducibility">whether or not the original findings are confirmed or could get reproduced</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Museums">Museums</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Museums"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Metadata in a museum context is the information that trained cultural documentation specialists, such as <a href="/wiki/Archivist" title="Archivist">archivists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Librarian" title="Librarian">librarians</a>, museum <a href="/wiki/Registrar_(museum)" class="mw-redirect" title="Registrar (museum)">registrars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Curator" title="Curator">curators</a>, create to index, structure, describe, identify, or otherwise specify works of art, architecture, cultural objects and their images.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Descriptive metadata is most commonly used in museum contexts for object identification and resource recovery purposes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Usage">Usage</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Usage"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Metadata is developed and applied within collecting institutions and museums in order to:
</p>
<ul><li>Facilitate resource discovery and execute search queries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Create digital archives that store information relating to various aspects of museum collections and cultural objects, and serve archival and managerial purposes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Provide public audiences access to cultural objects through publishing digital content online.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Standards_2">Standards</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Standards"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many museums and cultural heritage centers recognize that given the diversity of artworks and cultural objects, no single model or standard suffices to describe and catalog cultural works.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, a sculpted Indigenous artifact could be classified as an artwork, an archaeological artifact, or an Indigenous heritage item. The early stages of standardization in archiving, description and cataloging within the museum community began in the late 1990s with the development of standards such as <a href="/wiki/Categories_for_the_Description_of_Works_of_Art" title="Categories for the Description of Works of Art">Categories for the Description of Works of Art</a> (CDWA), Spectrum, <a href="/wiki/CIDOC_Conceptual_Reference_Model" title="CIDOC Conceptual Reference Model">CIDOC Conceptual Reference Model</a> (CRM), Cataloging Cultural Objects (CCO) and the CDWA Lite XML schema.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These standards use <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a> and <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a> markup languages for machine processing, publication and implementation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anglo-American_Cataloguing_Rules" title="Anglo-American Cataloguing Rules">Anglo-American Cataloguing Rules</a> (AACR), originally developed for characterizing books, have also been applied to cultural objects, works of art and architecture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Standards, such as the CCO, are integrated within a Museum's <a href="/wiki/Collections_Management_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Collections Management System">Collections Management System</a> (CMS), a database through which museums are able to manage their collections, acquisitions, loans and conservation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Scholars and professionals in the field note that the "quickly evolving landscape of standards and technologies" creates challenges for cultural documentarians, specifically non-technically trained professionals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2016)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Most collecting institutions and museums use a <a href="/wiki/Relational_database" title="Relational database">relational database</a> to categorize cultural works and their images.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Relational databases and metadata work to document and describe the complex relationships amongst cultural objects and multi-faceted works of art, as well as between objects and places, people, and artistic movements.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Relational database structures are also beneficial within collecting institutions and museums because they allow for archivists to make a clear distinction between cultural objects and their images; an unclear distinction could lead to confusing and inaccurate searches.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Cultural_objects">Cultural objects</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Cultural objects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An object's materiality, function, and purpose, as well as the size (e.g., measurements, such as height, width, weight), storage requirements (e.g., climate-controlled environment), and focus of the museum and collection, influence the descriptive depth of the data attributed to the object by cultural documentarians.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The established institutional cataloging practices, goals, and expertise of cultural documentarians and database structure also influence the information ascribed to cultural objects and the ways in which cultural objects are categorized.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, museums often employ standardized commercial collection management software that prescribes and limits the ways in which archivists can describe artworks and cultural objects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As well, collecting institutions and museums use <a href="/wiki/Controlled_vocabulary" title="Controlled vocabulary">Controlled Vocabularies</a> to describe cultural objects and artworks in their collections.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Getty Vocabularies and the Library of Congress Controlled Vocabularies are reputable within the museum community and are recommended by CCO standards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Museums are encouraged to use controlled vocabularies that are contextual and relevant to their collections and enhance the functionality of their digital information systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Controlled Vocabularies are beneficial within databases because they provide a high level of consistency, improving resource retrieval.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Metadata structures, including controlled vocabularies, reflect the <a href="/wiki/Ontology_(information_science)" title="Ontology (information science)">ontologies</a> of the systems from which they were created. Often the processes through which cultural objects are described and categorized through metadata in museums do not reflect the perspectives of the maker communities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Online_content">Online content</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Online content"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Metadata has been instrumental in the creation of digital information systems and archives within museums and has made it easier for museums to publish digital content online. This has enabled audiences who might not have had access to cultural objects due to geographic or economic barriers to have access to them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the 2000s, as more museums have adopted archival standards and created intricate databases, discussions about <a href="/wiki/Linked_data" title="Linked data">Linked Data</a> between museum databases have come up in the museum, archival, and library science communities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Collection Management Systems (CMS) and <a href="/wiki/Digital_asset_management" title="Digital asset management">Digital Asset Management</a> tools can be local or shared systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;81-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Digital_humanities" title="Digital humanities">Digital Humanities</a> scholars note many benefits of interoperability between museum databases and collections, while also acknowledging the difficulties of achieving such interoperability.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Law">Law</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Law"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1305433154" /><table class="box-Globalize plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="Globe icon." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/60px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" decoding="async" width="48" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/120px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="290" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this article <b>may not represent a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the subject</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You may <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a>, discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Metadata" title="Talk:Metadata">talk page</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_wizard" title="Wikipedia:Article wizard">create a new article</a>, as appropriate.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2015</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="United_States">United States</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: United States"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Problems involving metadata in <a href="/wiki/Litigation" class="mw-redirect" title="Litigation">litigation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> are becoming widespread.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2011)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Courts have looked at various questions involving metadata, including the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_(law)" title="Discovery (law)">discoverability</a> of metadata by parties. The Federal Rules of Civil Procedure have specific rules for discovery of electronically stored information, and subsequent case law applying those rules has elucidated on the litigant's duty to produce metadata when litigating in federal court.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In October 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Supreme_Court" title="Arizona Supreme Court">Arizona Supreme Court</a> has ruled that metadata records are <a href="/wiki/Public_record" class="mw-redirect" title="Public record">public record</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Document metadata have proven particularly important in legal environments in which litigation has requested metadata, that can include sensitive information detrimental to a certain party in court. Using <a href="/wiki/Metadata_removal_tool" title="Metadata removal tool">metadata removal tools</a> to "clean" or redact documents can mitigate the risks of unwittingly sending sensitive data. This process partially (see <a href="/wiki/Data_remanence" title="Data remanence">data remanence</a>) protects law firms from potentially damaging leaking of sensitive data through <a href="/wiki/Electronic_discovery" title="Electronic discovery">electronic discovery</a>.
</p><p>Opinion polls have shown that 45% of Americans are "not at all confident" in the ability of social media sites to ensure their personal data is secure and 40% say that social media sites should not be able to store any information on individuals. 76% of Americans say that they are not confident that the information advertising agencies collect on them is secure and 50% say that online advertising agencies should not be allowed to record any of their information at all.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="European_Union">European Union</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: European Union"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the EU, the <a href="/wiki/Snowden_disclosures" title="Snowden disclosures">Snowden disclosures</a> have been influential in its review of matters concerning privacy and the processing of personal data.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of 2025, of the 18 European countries surveyed by the <a href="/wiki/Data_Retention_Directive" title="Data Retention Directive">Data Retention Directive</a>, only Germany, the Netherlands and Romania do not have any data retention rules in force.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 October 2020, the Court of Justice for the European Union (CJEU) ruled that indiscriminate mass data retention schemes are illegal under EU law.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With France passing a law on 18 December 2013 that facilitates the collection of data by the French military and intelligence services.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While on April 2022 the Portuguese Constitutional Court declared unconstitutional the CJEU laws on the retention of data generated or processed in connection with the provision of publicly available electronic communications services or of public communications networks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Australia">Australia</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Australia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In Australia, the need to strengthen national security has resulted in the introduction of a new metadata storage law.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This new law means that both security and policing agencies will be allowed to access up to 2 years of an individual's metadata, with the aim of making it easier to stop any terrorist attacks and serious crimes from happening.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Legislation">Legislation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Legislation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Legislative metadata has been the subject of some discussion in <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://public.resource.org/law.gov/">law.gov</a> forums such as workshops held by the <a href="/wiki/Legal_Information_Institute" title="Legal Information Institute">Legal Information Institute</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cornell_Law_School" title="Cornell Law School">Cornell Law School</a> on 22 and 23 March 2010. The documentation for these forums is titled, "Suggested metadata practices for legislation and regulations".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A handful of key points have been outlined by these discussions, section headings of which are listed as follows:
</p>
<ul><li>General Considerations</li>
<li>Document Structure</li>
<li>Document Contents</li>
<li>Metadata (elements of)</li>
<li>Layering</li>
<li>Point-in-time versus post-hoc</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Healthcare">Healthcare</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Healthcare"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Australian medical research pioneered the definition of metadata for applications in health care. That approach offers the first recognized attempt to adhere to international standards in medical sciences instead of defining a proprietary standard under the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) umbrella. The medical community yet did not approve of the need to follow metadata standards despite research that supported these standards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Biomedical_researches">Biomedical researches</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Biomedical researches"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Research studies in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Biomedicine" title="Biomedicine">biomedicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a> frequently yield large quantities of data, including results of <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> or <a href="/wiki/Metagenomics" title="Metagenomics">meta-genome</a> <a href="/wiki/Sequencing" title="Sequencing">sequencing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proteomics" title="Proteomics">proteomics</a> data, and even notes or plans created during the course of research itself.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Each data type involves its own variety of metadata and the processes necessary to produce these metadata. General metadata standards, such as ISA-Tab,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> allow researchers to create and exchange experimental metadata in consistent formats. Specific experimental approaches frequently have their own metadata standards and systems: metadata standards for <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a> include <a href="/wiki/MzML" class="mw-redirect" title="MzML">mzML</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and SPLASH,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a>-based standards such as <a href="/wiki/Protein_Data_Bank" title="Protein Data Bank">PDBML</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and SRA XML<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> serve as standards for macromolecular structure and sequencing data, respectively.
</p><p>The products of biomedical research are generally realized as peer-reviewed manuscripts and these publications are yet another source of data <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033199720">.mw-parser-output div.crossreference{padding-left:0}</style><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable crossreference selfref">(see <a href="#Science">#Science</a>)</span>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Data_warehousing">Data warehousing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Data warehousing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Data_warehouse" title="Data warehouse">data warehouse</a> (DW) is a repository of an organization's electronically stored data. Data warehouses are designed to manage and store the data. Data warehouses differ from <a href="/wiki/Business_intelligence" title="Business intelligence">business intelligence</a> (BI) systems because BI systems are designed to use data to create reports and analyze the information, to provide strategic guidance to management.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Metadata is an important tool in how data is stored in data warehouses. The purpose of a data warehouse is to house standardized, structured, consistent, integrated, correct, "cleaned" and timely data, extracted from various operational systems in an organization. The extracted data are integrated in the data warehouse environment to provide an enterprise-wide perspective. Data are structured in a way to serve the reporting and analytic requirements. The design of structural metadata commonality using a <a href="/wiki/Data_modeling" title="Data modeling">data modeling</a> method such as <a href="/wiki/Entity-relationship_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Entity-relationship model">entity-relationship model</a> diagramming is important in any data warehouse development effort. They detail metadata on each piece of data in the data warehouse. An essential component of a <a href="/wiki/Data_warehouse" title="Data warehouse">data warehouse</a>/<a href="/wiki/Business_intelligence" title="Business intelligence">business intelligence</a> system is the metadata and tools to manage and retrieve the metadata. <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Kimball" title="Ralph Kimball">Ralph Kimball</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> describes metadata as the DNA of the data warehouse as metadata defines the elements of the <a href="/wiki/Data_warehouse" title="Data warehouse">data warehouse</a> and how they work together.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ralph_Kimball" title="Ralph Kimball">Kimball</a> et al.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> refers to 3 main categories of metadata: Technical metadata, business metadata and process metadata. Technical metadata is primarily <a href="/wiki/Definition" title="Definition">definitional</a>, while business metadata and process metadata is primarily <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptive</a>. The categories sometimes overlap.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Technical metadata</b> defines the objects and processes in a DW/BI system, as seen from a technical point of view. The technical metadata includes the system metadata, which defines the data structures such as tables, fields, data types, indexes, and partitions in the relational engine, as well as databases, dimensions, measures, and data mining models. Technical metadata defines the data model and the way it is displayed for the users, with the reports, schedules, distribution lists, and user security rights.</li>
<li><b>Business metadata</b> is content from the data warehouse described in more user-friendly terms. The business metadata tells you what data you have, where they come from, what they mean and what their relationship is to other data in the data warehouse. Business metadata may also serve as documentation for the DW/BI system. Users who browse the data warehouse are primarily viewing the business metadata.</li>
<li><b>Process metadata</b> is used to describe the results of various operations in the data warehouse. Within the <a href="/wiki/Extract,_transform,_load" title="Extract, transform, load">ETL</a> process, all key data from tasks is logged on execution. This includes start time, end time, CPU seconds used, disk reads, disk writes, and rows processed. When troubleshooting the ETL or <a href="/wiki/Information_retrieval" title="Information retrieval">query</a> process, this sort of data becomes valuable. Process metadata is the fact measurement when building and using a DW/BI system. Some organizations make a living out of collecting and selling this sort of data to companies – in that case, the process metadata becomes the business metadata for the fact and dimension tables. Collecting process metadata is in the interest of business people who can use the data to identify the users of their products, which products they are using, and what level of service they are receiving.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Internet">Internet</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Internet"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a> format used to define web pages allows for the inclusion of a variety of types of metadata, from basic descriptive text, dates and keywords to further advanced metadata schemes such as the <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Core" title="Dublin Core">Dublin Core</a>, <a href="/wiki/E-GMS" title="E-GMS">e-GMS</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australian_Government_Locator_Service" title="Australian Government Locator Service">AGLS</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> standards. Pages and files can also be <a href="/wiki/Geotagging" title="Geotagging">geotagged</a> with <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">coordinates</a>, categorized or tagged, including collaboratively such as with <a href="/wiki/Folksonomy" title="Folksonomy">folksonomies</a>.
</p><p>When media has <a href="/wiki/Identifier" title="Identifier">identifiers</a> set or when such can be generated, information such as <a href="/wiki/Tag_(metadata)" title="Tag (metadata)">file tags</a> and descriptions can be pulled or <a href="/wiki/Web_scraping" title="Web scraping">scraped</a> from the Internet – for example about movies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Various online databases are aggregated and provide metadata for various data. The collaboratively built <a href="/wiki/Wikidata" title="Wikidata">Wikidata</a> has identifiers not just for media but also abstract concepts, various objects, and other entities, that can be looked up by humans and machines to retrieve useful information and to link knowledge in other knowledge bases and databases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10.1186/s12915-020-00940-y&#95;72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10.1186/s12915-020-00940-y-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Metadata may be included in the page's header or in a separate file. <a href="/wiki/Microformat" title="Microformat">Microformats</a> allow metadata to be added to on-page data in a way that regular web users do not see, but computers, <a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">web crawlers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Search_engine" title="Search engine">search engines</a> can readily access. Many search engines are cautious about using metadata in their ranking algorithms because of exploitation of metadata and the practice of search engine optimization, <a href="/wiki/Search_engine_optimization" title="Search engine optimization">SEO</a>, to improve rankings. See the <a href="/wiki/Meta_element" title="Meta element">Meta element</a> article for further discussion. This cautious attitude may be justified as people, according to Doctorow,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> are not executing care and diligence when creating their own metadata and that metadata is part of a competitive environment where the metadata is used to promote the metadata creators own purposes. Studies show that search engines respond to web pages with metadata implementations,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Google has an announcement on its site showing the meta tags that its search engine understands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Enterprise search startup <a href="/wiki/Swiftype" title="Swiftype">Swiftype</a> recognizes metadata as a relevance signal that webmasters can implement for their website-specific search engine, even releasing their own extension, known as Meta Tags 2.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Broadcast_industry">Broadcast industry</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Broadcast industry"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Broadcast" class="mw-redirect" title="Broadcast">broadcast</a> industry, metadata is linked to audio and video <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_media" class="mw-redirect" title="Broadcast media">broadcast media</a> to:
</p>
<ul><li><i>identify</i> the media: <a href="/wiki/Media_clip" title="Media clip">clip</a> or <a href="/wiki/Playlist" title="Playlist">playlist</a> names, duration, <a href="/wiki/Timecode" title="Timecode">timecode</a>, etc.</li>
<li><i>describe</i> the content: notes regarding the quality of video content, rating, description (for example, during a sport event, <a href="/wiki/Index_term" title="Index term">keywords</a> like <i>goal</i>, <i>red card</i> will be associated to some clips)</li>
<li><i>classify</i> media: metadata allows producers to sort the media or to easily and quickly find a video content (a <a href="/wiki/TV_news" class="mw-redirect" title="TV news">TV news</a> could urgently need some <a href="/wiki/Archiving" class="mw-redirect" title="Archiving">archive content</a> for a subject). For example, the BBC has a large subject classification system, <a href="/wiki/Lonclass" title="Lonclass">Lonclass</a>, a customized version of the more general-purpose <a href="/wiki/Universal_Decimal_Classification" title="Universal Decimal Classification">Universal Decimal Classification</a>.</li></ul>
<p>This metadata can be linked to the video media thanks to the <a href="/wiki/Video_server#Broadcast_automation" title="Video server">video servers</a>. Most major broadcast sporting events like <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> use this metadata to distribute their video content to <a href="/wiki/TV_station" class="mw-redirect" title="TV station">TV stations</a> through <a href="/wiki/Index_term" title="Index term">keywords</a>. It is often the host broadcaster<sup id="cite&#95;ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who is in charge of organizing metadata through its <i>International Broadcast Centre</i> and its video servers. This metadata is recorded with the images and entered by metadata operators (<i>loggers</i>) who associate in live metadata available in <i>metadata grids</i> through <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Multicam(LSM)" class="mw-redirect" title="Multicam(LSM)">Multicam(LSM)</a> or <a href="/wiki/IPDirector" title="IPDirector">IPDirector</a> used during the FIFA World Cup or Olympic Games).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ecology_and_environment">Ecology and environment</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Ecology and environment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Standardization#Environmental_protection" title="Standardization">Standardization §&#160;Environmental protection</a></div>
<p>Ecological and environmental metadata is intended to document the "who, what, when, where, why, and how" of data collection for a particular study. This typically means which organization or institution collected the data, what type of data, which date(s) the data was collected, the rationale for the data collection, and the methodology used for the data collection. Metadata should be generated in a format commonly used by the most relevant science community, such as <a href="/wiki/Darwin_Core" title="Darwin Core">Darwin Core</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecological_Metadata_Language" title="Ecological Metadata Language">Ecological Metadata Language</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Core" title="Dublin Core">Dublin Core</a>. Metadata editing tools exist to facilitate metadata generation (e.g. Metavist,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(metadata_search_system)" title="Mercury (metadata search system)">Mercury</a>, Morpho<sup id="cite&#95;ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>). Metadata should describe the <a href="/wiki/Data_provenance" class="mw-redirect" title="Data provenance">provenance</a> of the data (where they originated, as well as any transformations the data underwent) and how to give credit for (cite) the data products.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Digital_music">Digital music</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Digital music"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="#On_the_Internet">§&#160;On the Internet</a></div>
<p>When first released in 1982, Compact Discs only contained a Table Of Contents (TOC) with the number of tracks on the disc and their length in samples.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pohlmann1989&#95;117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pohlmann1989-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fourteen years later in 1996, a revision of the <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_Digital_Audio" title="Compact Disc Digital Audio">CD Red Book</a> standard added <a href="/wiki/CD-Text" title="CD-Text">CD-Text</a> to carry additional metadata.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> But CD-Text was not widely adopted. Shortly thereafter, it became common for personal computers to retrieve metadata from external sources (e.g. <a href="/wiki/CDDB" title="CDDB">CDDB</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gracenote" title="Gracenote">Gracenote</a>) based on the TOC.
</p><p>Digital <a href="/wiki/Sound_recording_and_reproduction" title="Sound recording and reproduction">audio</a> formats such as <a href="/wiki/Digital_audio_file" class="mw-redirect" title="Digital audio file">digital audio files</a> superseded music formats such as <a href="/wiki/Cassette_tape" title="Cassette tape">cassette tapes</a> and <a href="/wiki/CDs" class="mw-redirect" title="CDs">CDs</a> in the 2000s. Digital audio files could be labeled with more information than could be contained in just the file name. That descriptive information is called the <b>audio tag</b> or audio metadata in general. Computer programs specializing in adding or modifying this information are called <a href="/wiki/Tag_editor" title="Tag editor">tag editors</a>. Metadata can be used to name, describe, catalog, and indicate ownership or copyright for a digital audio file, and its presence makes it much easier to locate a specific audio file within a group, typically through use of a search engine that accesses the metadata. As different digital audio formats were developed, attempts were made to standardize a specific location within the digital files where this information could be stored.
</p><p>As a result, almost all digital audio formats, including <a href="/wiki/Mp3" class="mw-redirect" title="Mp3">mp3</a>, broadcast wav, and <a href="/wiki/Audio_Interchange_File_Format" title="Audio Interchange File Format">AIFF</a> files, have similar standardized locations that can be populated with metadata. The metadata for compressed and uncompressed digital music is often encoded in the <a href="/wiki/ID3" title="ID3">ID3</a> tag. Common editors such as <a href="/wiki/TagLib" title="TagLib">TagLib</a> support MP3, Ogg Vorbis, FLAC, MPC, Speex, WavPack TrueAudio, WAV, AIFF, MP4, and ASF file formats.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cloud_applications">Cloud applications</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Cloud applications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>With the availability of <a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing" title="Cloud computing">cloud</a> applications, which include those to add metadata to content, metadata is increasingly available over the Internet.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Administration_and_management">Administration and management</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Administration and management"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Storage">Storage</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Storage"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Metadata can be stored either <i>internally</i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-id3&#95;119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-id3-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in the same file or structure as the data (this is also called <i>embedded metadata</i>), or <i>externally</i>, in a separate file or field from the described data. A data repository typically stores the metadata <i>detached</i> from the data but can be designed to support embedded metadata approaches. Each option has advantages and disadvantages:
</p>
<ul><li>Internal storage means metadata always travels as part of the data they describe; thus, metadata is always available with the data, and can be manipulated locally. This method creates redundancy (precluding normalization), and does not allow managing all of a system's metadata in one place. It arguably increases consistency, since the metadata is readily changed whenever the data is changed.</li>
<li>External storage allows collocating metadata for all the contents, for example in a database, for more efficient searching and management. Redundancy can be avoided by normalizing the metadata's organization. In this approach, metadata can be united with the content when information is transferred, for example in <a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">Streaming media</a>; or can be referenced (for example, as a web link) from the transferred content. On the downside, the division of the metadata from the data content, especially in standalone files that refer to their source metadata elsewhere, increases the opportunities for misalignments between the two, as changes to either may not be reflected in the other.</li></ul>
<p>Metadata can be stored in either human-readable or binary form. Storing metadata in a human-readable format such as <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a> can be useful because users can understand and edit it without specialized tools.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sutter&#95;120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutter-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, text-based formats are rarely optimized for storage capacity, communication time, or processing speed. A binary metadata format enables efficiency in all these respects, but requires special software to convert the binary information into human-readable content.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Database_management">Database management</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Database management"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Each relational database system has its own mechanisms for storing metadata. Examples of relational-database metadata include:
</p>
<ul><li>Tables of all tables in a database, their names, sizes, and number of rows in each table.</li>
<li>Tables of columns in each database, what tables they are used in, and the type of data stored in each column.</li></ul>
<p>In database terminology, this set of metadata is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Database_catalog" title="Database catalog">catalog</a>. The <a href="/wiki/SQL" title="SQL">SQL</a> standard specifies a uniform means to access the catalog, called the <a href="/wiki/Information_schema" title="Information schema">information schema</a>, but not all databases implement it, even if they implement other aspects of the SQL standard. For an example of database-specific metadata access methods, see <a href="/wiki/Oracle_metadata" title="Oracle metadata">Oracle metadata</a>. Programmatic access to metadata is possible using APIs such as <a href="/wiki/JDBC" class="mw-redirect" title="JDBC">JDBC</a>, or SchemaCrawler.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schemacrawler&#95;121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schemacrawler-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Popular_culture">Popular culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One of the first satirical examinations of the concept of Metadata as we understand it today is American science fiction author <a href="/wiki/Hal_Draper" title="Hal Draper">Hal Draper</a>'s short story, "<a href="/wiki/MS_Fnd_in_a_Lbry" title="MS Fnd in a Lbry">MS Fnd in a Lbry</a>" (1961). Here, the knowledge of all Mankind is condensed into an object the size of a desk drawer, however, the magnitude of the metadata (e.g. catalog of catalogs of... , as well as indexes and histories) eventually leads to dire yet humorous consequences for the human race. The story prefigures the modern consequences of allowing metadata to become more important than the real data it is concerned with, and the risks inherent in that eventuality as a cautionary tale.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="The_Difference_between_Metadata_and_Documentation">The Difference between Metadata and Documentation</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metadata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: The Difference between Metadata and Documentation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Documentation" title="Documentation">Documentation</a> refers to all contextual information that explains and describes a dataset, system, or research process to ensure it is understandable, usable, and reusable. It covers broad details such as the study background, data collection methods, file inventories, codebooks, data dictionaries, user guides, and workflow notes. In contrast, metadata is a specific and highly structured form of documentation that provides standardized information about the data itself—such as the title, creator, file format, size, access conditions, and how files or sections are organized. While documentation gives the full narrative and instructions surrounding the data, metadata offers concise, structured descriptors that help users and systems identify, locate, manage, and preserve the data efficiently.
</p><p>Metadata and documentation are two different things, but are related and distinct concepts in information management. Metadata refers to data about data, which provides structured information that describes the characteristics, context, and attributes of a data set or digital resource. It helps in organizing, finding, and understanding data by offering details such as author, date, format, and keywords. Documentation, on the other hand, consists detailed written instructions, guides, or manuals that explain how to use, interpret, or maintain a system, software, or data resource. While metadata enhances discoverability and management of data, documentation offers a comprehensive explanation and instructions, which ensures users that they can effectively utilize and understand the resource. Both play crucial roles in data governance, usability, and preservation.
</p>
